    <meta name="dc.description" content="This work describes the fabrication of a novel one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO2 nanorods encased in onion-like carbon (or) carbon nano-onions (OLC) via microwave irradiation techniques employing electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD), which is especially beneficial for rapid ion and electron transfer, and great structural stability. The composite of α-MnO2 and OLC demonstrates exceptional performance as an electrode across various electrochemical energy storage systems, including zinc-ion batteries (ZIB), sodium-ion batteries (SIB), and supercapacitors (SC) than the pristine α-MnO2. In SIB systems, the composite exhibits a specific capacity of 266 mAh g−1 at initial cycle with 50% capacity retention over 500 cycles, whereas the pristine electrode delivers only 39% capacity retention. The rapid yet controlled charge transfer kinetics facilitated by OLC addition in the α-MnO2 matrix outperforms as the ZIB cathode with an excellent specific capacity of 476 mAh g−1 with 100% capacity retention, while the pristine sample exhibits 77.5% capacity retention. As a SC electrode, the α-MnO2/OLC composite exhibits better electrochemical properties such as rectangular behavior, increased specific capacitance (792 F g−1), excellent capacity retention at high current densities, and others. The higher surface area that could be offered by the OLC to the α-MnO2 matrix facilitates the improved electrochemistry in the pristine sample and this kind of modification can be a viable solution to overcome the limitations of α-MnO2 for electrochemical energy storage applications. It is important to note that the performance outputs of α-MnO2/OLC composite are far better than the regular carbon (graphite, graphene) in α-MnO2 electrodes. Further, OLC provided with high surface area and ordered morphology can play the role of conductivity booster, structural stabilizer, and electrochemical active material in all the energy storage applications which may give a significant research attention in near future.">

    <meta property="og:url" content="https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2">
    <meta property="og:type" content="article">
    <meta property="og:site_name" content="SpringerLink">
    <meta property="og:title" content="Enhanced electrochemical energy storage devices utilizing a one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO2 nanocomposite encased in onion-like carbon - Journal of Materials Science">
    <meta property="og:description" content="This work describes the fabrication of a novel one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO2 nanorods encased in onion-like carbon (or) carbon nano-onions (OLC) via microwave irradiation techniques employing electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD), which is especially beneficial for rapid ion and electron transfer, and great structural stability. The composite of α-MnO2 and OLC demonstrates exceptional performance as an electrode across various electrochemical energy storage systems, including zinc-ion batteries (ZIB), sodium-ion batteries (SIB), and supercapacitors (SC) than the pristine α-MnO2. In SIB systems, the composite exhibits a specific capacity of 266 mAh g−1 at initial cycle with 50% capacity retention over 500 cycles, whereas the pristine electrode delivers only 39% capacity retention. The rapid yet controlled charge transfer kinetics facilitated by OLC addition in the α-MnO2 matrix outperforms as the ZIB cathode with an excellent specific capacity of 476 mAh g−1 with 100% capacity retention, while the pristine sample exhibits 77.5% capacity retention. As a SC electrode, the α-MnO2/OLC composite exhibits better electrochemical properties such as rectangular behavior, increased specific capacitance (792 F g−1), excellent capacity retention at high current densities, and others. The higher surface area that could be offered by the OLC to the α-MnO2 matrix facilitates the improved electrochemistry in the pristine sample and this kind of modification can be a viable solution to overcome the limitations of α-MnO2 for electrochemical energy storage applications. It is important to note that the performance outputs of α-MnO2/OLC composite are far better than the regular carbon (graphite, graphene) in α-MnO2 electrodes. Further, OLC provided with high surface area and ordered morphology can play the role of conductivity booster, structural stabilizer, and electrochemical active material in all the energy storage applications which may give a significant research attention in near future.">
    <meta property="og:image" content="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig1_HTML.png">

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Enhanced electrochemical energy storage devices utilizing a one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO2 nanocomposite encased in onion-like carbon","description":"This work describes the fabrication of a novel one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO2 nanorods encased in onion-like carbon (or) carbon nano-onions (OLC) via microwave irradiation techniques employing electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD), which is especially beneficial for rapid ion and electron transfer, and great structural stability. The composite of α-MnO2 and OLC demonstrates exceptional performance as an electrode across various electrochemical energy storage systems, including zinc-ion batteries (ZIB), sodium-ion batteries (SIB), and supercapacitors (SC) than the pristine α-MnO2. In SIB systems, the composite exhibits a specific capacity of 266 mAh g−1 at initial cycle with 50% capacity retention over 500 cycles, whereas the pristine electrode delivers only 39% capacity retention. The rapid yet controlled charge transfer kinetics facilitated by OLC addition in the α-MnO2 matrix outperforms as the ZIB cathode with an excellent specific capacity of 476 mAh g−1 with 100% capacity retention, while the pristine sample exhibits 77.5% capacity retention. As a SC electrode, the α-MnO2/OLC composite exhibits better electrochemical properties such as rectangular behavior, increased specific capacitance (792 F g−1), excellent capacity retention at high current densities, and others. The higher surface area that could be offered by the OLC to the α-MnO2 matrix facilitates the improved electrochemistry in the pristine sample and this kind of modification can be a viable solution to overcome the limitations of α-MnO2 for electrochemical energy storage applications. It is important to note that the performance outputs of α-MnO2/OLC composite are far better than the regular carbon (graphite, graphene) in α-MnO2 electrodes. Further, OLC provided with high surface area and ordered morphology can play the role of conductivity booster, structural stabilizer, and electrochemical active material in all the energy storage applications which may give a significant research attention in near future.","datePublished":"2024-03-27T00:00:00Z","dateModified":"2024-03-27T00:00:00Z","pageStart":"5450","pageEnd":"5469","license":"http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/","sameAs":"https://doi.org/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2","keywords":["Materials Science","general","Characterization and Evaluation of Materials","Polymer Sciences","Solid Mechanics","Crystallography and Scattering Methods","Classical Mechanics"],"image":["https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig1_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig2_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig3_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig4_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig5_HTML.png","https://media.springernature.com/lw1200/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig6_HTML.png"],"isPartOf":{"name":"Journal of Materials Science","issn":["1573-4803","0022-2461"],"volumeNumber":"59","@type":["Periodical","PublicationVolume"]},"publisher":{"name":"Springer US","logo":{"url":"https://www.springernature.com/app-sn/public/images/logo-springernature.png","@type":"ImageObject"},"@type":"Organization"},"author":[{"name":"N.                 <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                    
                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>This work describes the fabrication of a novel one-dimensional (1D) α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods encased in onion-like carbon (or) carbon nano-onions (OLC) via microwave irradiation techniques employing electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD), which is especially beneficial for rapid ion and electron transfer, and great structural stability. The composite of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and OLC demonstrates exceptional performance as an electrode across various electrochemical energy storage systems, including zinc-ion batteries (ZIB), sodium-ion batteries (SIB), and supercapacitors (SC) than the pristine α-MnO<sub>2</sub>. In SIB systems, the composite exhibits a specific capacity of 266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at initial cycle with 50% capacity retention over 500 cycles, whereas the pristine electrode delivers only 39% capacity retention. The rapid yet controlled charge transfer kinetics facilitated by OLC addition in the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> matrix outperforms as the ZIB cathode with an excellent specific capacity of 476 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with 100% capacity retention, while the pristine sample exhibits 77.5% capacity retention. As a SC electrode, the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite exhibits better electrochemical properties such as rectangular behavior, increased specific capacitance (792 F g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent capacity retention at high current densities, and others. The higher surface area that could be offered by the OLC to the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> matrix facilitates the improved electrochemistry in the pristine sample and this kind of modification can be a viable solution to overcome the limitations of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> for electrochemical energy storage applications. It is important to note that the performance outputs of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite are far better than the regular carbon (graphite, graphene) in α-MnO<sub>2</sub> electrodes. Further, OLC provided with high surface area and ordered morphology can play the role of conductivity booster, structural stabilizer, and electrochemical active material in all the energy storage applications which may give a significant research attention in near future.</p></div></div></section>

                                <div class="main-content">
                                    <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Significant efforts have been made in recent years to develop high-power and energy-density storage devices that can be used in portable electronic devices, smart grids, and electric vehicles to meet the current requirement of zero-carbon or CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. In this purview, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are traditionally viewed as the viable solutions to meet out the energy demands still the present era faces the negative aspects of this technology. A few to mention are: scarcity, safety issues, relying on specific electrode components due to the compatibility issues. Owing to these, it is high time to explore the newer battery grade materials which fit into the different chemistry of electrochemical energy storage mechanisms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nithyadharseni P, Reddy MV, Fanny H, Adams S, Chowdari BVR (2015) Facile one-pot synthesis and Li-cycling properties of MnO2. The emerging technologies with promising large-scale applications are sodium-ion batteries (SIB), Zn-ion batteries (ZIB), Mg/K/Al-ion batteries, supercapacitors (SC), fuel cells, etc. This research work exclusively focuses on the cathode component of high performing systems SIB, ZIB, and SC. Yet, the significant challenge is in identifying the suitable electrode materials which effectively intercalates/alloys with the Na<sup>+</sup> ions due to the ionic radii issues of sodium. Various cathode materials have been investigated, including fluoride-based materials, fluorophosphate, fluorosulfates, layered transition metal oxides, Olivine, and NASICONs; however, none of the above cathode materials demonstrated suitable electrochemical performance in terms of capacity, cyclability, and rate capability. In this series, manganese dioxides possess excellent structural properties which has enough room to accommodate the larger cations in its tunnel structure during redox reactions. Aqueous ion batteries are traditional electrochemical systems yet getting accelerated in this twenty-first century owing to the above-mentioned concerns. Particularly, ZIB are astounding due to their performance in aqueous electrolytes, exceptional safety, harmlessness to the environmental, abundance of Zn, and high specific power of the systems for stationary applications. Aqueous systems demand high volume of the electrolyte solution for high performance, and hence, these systems are ideal for stationary applications. Specifically, when compared to LIBs, Zn has a high volumetric and gravimetric specific capacity of 5855 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> and 820 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Karbak M, Baazizi M, Sayah S, Lambert CA, Tison Y, Martinez H, Chafik T, Ghamouss F (2023) Unraveling high-performance oxygen-deficient amorphous manganese oxide as the cathode for advanced zinc ion batteries. The only challenge in ZIB systems is the dendrite growth on repeated charge–discharge cycles, while this can be overcome with the suitable battery components. Layered structures with large interlayer spacing or tunnel structures with multiple ion channels are highly considered cathode materials for ZIBs. In this case, α-MnO<sub>2</sub> has been considered a promising material and has attracted significant interest due to its massive [2 × 2] and [1 × 1] tunnel structures along its c-axis made of four edge-sharing MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral units [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang Z, Li S, Wang X, Wen Z, Sun J (2021) Single-atom platinum implanted on manganese dioxide boosted the high-rate performance of cathodes for zinc-ion batteries. The power density is the driving force in most of electronics and automotive industries which needs different electrochemistry than the batteries. This way, SCs have drawn a lot of interest due to their distinctive features, including high power, long cycle life, and an environmentally friendly design. They serve as a bridge for the energy-power gap between a traditional capacitor (with high power) and fuel cells/batteries (having high energy storage) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iamprasertkun P, Krittayavathananon A, Seubsai A, Chanlek N, Kidkhunthod P, Sangthong W, Maensiri S, Yimnirun R, Nilmoung S, Pannopard P, Ittisanronnachai S, Kongpatpanich K, Limtrakul J, Sawangphruk M (2016) Charge storage mechanisms of manganese oxide nanosheets and N-doped reduced graphene oxide aerogel for high-performance asymmetric supercapacitors. J Power Sources 234:302–309" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e622">40</a>]. The electrode materials with high surface area are the ideal materials for SC applications to undergo electrostatic reactions rather than the electrochemical reactions. Metal oxide materials are currently gaining popularity due to their inherent properties such as high specific capacitance and conductivity. Until now, a wide range of metal oxides such as RuO<sub>2</sub>, MnO<sub>2</sub>, NiO, IrO<sub>2</sub>, SnO<sub>2</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and others have been extensively studied [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu CC, Hung CY, Chang KH, Yang YL (2011) A hierarchical nanostructure consisting of amorphous MnO2, Mn3O4 nanocrystallites, and single-crystalline MnOOH nanowires for supercapacitors. J Mater Sci 57:8818–8827" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e641">53</a>]. Among them, α-MnO<sub>2</sub> has been extensively studied due to its unique properties of the high theoretical capacitance of 1300 F g<sup>−1</sup>; however, its physical and chemical factors and microstructure can affect the pseudocapacitive behavior in terms of cycling stability and specific capacitance. Also α-MnO<sub>2</sub> possesses poor electronic conductivity (10<sup>−5</sup>–10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and low ionic diffusion constant (~ 10<sup>−13</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;V&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) which makes it imperative to modify this component for viable applications.</p><p>Following this, numerous reports have been made on morphological variations of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> such as nanorods, nanowires, nanotubes, and nanofibers for rapid Faradaic reactions, short diffusion paths for electron transport, and high surface area, which have resulted in improved charge storage capabilities [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Palaniyandy N, Reddy MV, Mamba BB (2023) Unveiling the electrochemistry effect on microsphere and nanorod morphology of NaSn2(PO4)3 anode for lithium/sodium batteries. J Solid State Electrochem 27(2):427–438. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10008-022-05324-6
                  
                " href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lakshmi D, Diana MI, Manoj G, Jayapandi S, Nalini B, Selvin PC, Balraju P (2022) Influence of reducing agents in nanoparticle synthesis: SnO2-ZnO composite anode. Appl Organomet Chem. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1002/aoc.6714
                  
                " href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_1">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yue T, Shen B, Gao P (2022) Carbon material/MnO2 as conductive skeleton for supercapacitor electrode material: a review. Renew Sustain Energy Rev 158:112131" href="#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_2">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bhat TS, Jadhav SA, Beknalkar SA, Patil SS, Patil PS (2022) MnO2 core-shell type materials for high-performance supercapacitors: a short review. Inorg Chem Commun 141:109493" href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_3">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kumar N, Gajraj V, Upadhyay S, Chetana S, Sankaranarayanan S, Hossain I, Joshi NC, Priyadarshi N, Sen A (2023) One-step fragmentation of a 2D MXene across the fine 1D MnO2 surface and its supercapacitance. CrystEngComm 25:72–85" href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_4">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mustaqeem M, Naikoo GA, Yarmohammadi M, Pedram MZ, Pourfarzad H, Dar RA, Taha SA, Hassan IU, Bhat MY, Chen YF (2022) Rational design of metal oxide-based electrode materials for high-performance supercapacitors–A review. J Energy Storage 55:105419" href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_5">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abdelrahim AM, Abd El-Moghny MG, El-Shakre ME, El-Deab MS (2023) High mass loading MnO2/graphite felt electrode with marked stability over a wide potential window of 1.9 V for supercapacitor application. J Energy Storage 57:106218" href="#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e669_6">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="McDonough JK, Gogotsi Y (2013) Carbon onions: synthesis and electrochemical applications. Electrochem Soc Interface 22:61–66" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e672">61</a>]. Further, α-MnO<sub>2</sub> has been reported with different dopants, composites, and other anionic modifications as well. The modification factors rely on the target applications such as improving the conductivity, electrochemical performance, stability, and other factors. The present research work aims at batteries and SC applications which require better surface area and electrical conductivity for effective charge migration, electrolyte percolation, and rate capability and hence including carbon in the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> matrix could be the ideal problem to resolve. Among different allotropes of carbon such as fullerene, carbon nanotubes, graphite and graphene, and onion-like carbons (OLC) are the carbon nanoparticles possessing exceptional small size (or zero dimension). OLCs can be added to any electrode material as conductivity boosters as they easily disperse in the target matrix than the other carbon (1D and 2D) materials [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Zeiger M, Jäckel N, Mochalic VN, Presser V (2016) Review: carbon onions for electrochemical energy storage. J Mater Chem A 4:3172–3196" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e680">62</a>]. Regarding the structure of the OLCs, they have concentric shells with or without a core of nano-diamond particles and hence always termed as multiwall structures [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lakshmi D, Diana MI, Jayapandi S, Selvin PC, Balraju P (2023) Deciphering the functionalization routes for SnO2 anodes. J Mater Sci 58:15471–15484. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s10853-023-08986-3
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e683">63</a>]. Due to the extremely smaller dimension and higher temperature range during the synthesis, OLCs have lesser intrinsic porosity while owing to the internal layered arrangements; surface porosity can be exploited for the target applications. In the present study, α-MnO<sub>2</sub> prepared by microwave irradiation method and appropriate mixing of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and OLC for composite preparation through ultra-sonication is reported. The physical and chemical properties of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite and the pristine material are studied. The electrochemical performance of versatile 1D α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC nanocomposite on SIBs, aqueous ZIBs, and SCs is studied and comparatively reported with the pristine.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Experimental section"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Experimental section</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Preparation method of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> from electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD)</h3><p>First, the raw EMD (Delta EMD (Pty) Ltd., South Africa) was heated at 600&nbsp;°C for 22&nbsp;h under an air atmosphere to obtain spherical α-Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The α-Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was dispersed in 8&nbsp;M H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> solution for 5&nbsp;h using a magnetic stirrer. Then, the above solution was transferred into microwave tubes and processed for 20&nbsp;min at 1000 W and 280&nbsp;°C, constant temperature. The resultant microwaved solution was centrifuged and washed with distilled water several times until removes all the impurities and dried for 24&nbsp;h at 80&nbsp;°C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Preparation method of OLC from nano-diamond and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite</h3><p>OLC was produced from nano-diamond powder with a purity of 99% (purchased from Gelon LIB Co. LTD, China). The nano-diamond powder was added in a closed-lid cylindrical graphite crucible (30&nbsp;mm in diameter and 20&nbsp;mm in height) and thermally annealed at 1780&nbsp;°C for 3&nbsp;h in a water-cooled high-temperature vacuum furnace. For preparing α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite, α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods and the appropriate amount of OLC were dispersed in 40&nbsp;mL of ethanol and distilled water in a beaker. Then, the beaker was placed in an ultra-sonication and magnetic stirrer for 4&nbsp;h, after which samples were washed by centrifugation with copies amounts of de-ionized water. Finally, the resultant powder was heated at 80&nbsp;°C overnight to obtain α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Materials characterization and techniques</h3><p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) experiments were carried out on a Kratos Axis Ultra-DLD system (Shimadzu) with monochromated Al Kα radiation (1486.6&nbsp;eV). Binding energies were calibrated using the containment carbon (C 1&nbsp;s at 284.6&nbsp;eV). The spectra analysis was performed with the XPS Peak 4.1 program, and a Shirley function was used to subtract the background. Powder X-ray diffraction data were obtained using a Rigaku Ultima IV diffractometer system operating at <i>λ</i> = 0.15405&nbsp;nm as a radiation source operating at 40&nbsp;kV and 30&nbsp;mA with Cu-Kα radiation and scan range between 10 and 70°. Thermal behavior of the pristine and composite samples was tested through TG–DTA analysis in inert atmosphere using PerkinElmer TGA 4000 Thermogravimetric Analyzer. Scanning electron microscope was imaged using LEO 1525 field emission scanning microscope (FE-SEM) with an acceleration voltage of 2.00&nbsp;kV. The transmission electron microscopes (TEM) were carried out by using a JEOL HRJEM–2100 microscopy using LAB6 filament as an electron source. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectra (EDX) were recorded with an EDX system (Oxford Instruments) at 5 different positions. The chemical composition was calculated using the AZtec energy analysis software (Oxford Instruments). The BET (Brunauer, Emmett, and Teller) measurements were performed out using a Micromeritics TriStar II instrument.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Electrochemical characterization techniques</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">Sodium- and Zinc-ion batteries</h4><p>The electrode fabrication for SIB and ZIBs is as follows: The slurry was prepared using the mixture of active materials (α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite), conducting acetylene black and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF) as a polymer binder in the ratio of 75:15:10 wt% for SIB and 75:15:10 wt% for ZIB. The slurry was coated onto cleaned and polished aluminum foil/nickel foam disks as the current collector and then dried in a vacuum oven at 80&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h for SIB/ZIBs. The coated aluminum foil (mass loading of the electrodes are 3–4&nbsp;mg) was then cut into circular disks and kept inside the glove box filled with argon gas. The coin cells for sodium-ion batteries were assembled in an argon-filled glove box (MBraun-O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.5&nbsp;ppm), while the coin cells for ZIBs were fabricated outside at room temperature. The electrolyte of 1&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a 1:1 (v/v) mixture of EC: PC for SIBs and 1&nbsp;M ZnSO<sub>4</sub> with the additive of 0.1&nbsp;M MnSO<sub>4</sub> in distilled water for ZIB is used.</p><p>The sodium metal foil/zinc plate can be used as a reference/counter electrode, and the Celgard 2300 polypropylene-based membrane/fiberglass filter separator is used for SIBs and ZIBs. The assembled coin cells were aged for 12&nbsp;h prior to the electrochemical measurements. The galvanostatic cycling performances of the compound were carried out by using a MACCOR series 4000 tester. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) for fresh and cycled cells were analyzed by using a Bio-Logic VMP3 potentiostat/galvanostat controlled by EC-Lab v10.40 software.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec8">Supercapacitor</h4><p>Electrochemical measurements were performed on conventional two-electrode (full or symmetric cell) system configurations with α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite as working and reference electrodes, respectively, and 1&nbsp;M Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> aqueous solution as electrolyte. The active electrode materials (α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC), carbon black (CB) (PRINTEX XE-2-B, Degussa) as a conducting agent, and polyvinylidene fluoride powder (PVDF) (Sigma-Aldrich) as a binder were mixed uniformly using a pestle and mortar in the weight ratios of 80:15:5. A few ml of N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidone (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>9</sub>NO) (NMP) (Reagent Plus, 99%, Sigma-Aldrich) was added as a wetting agent to produce a homogeneous slurry. For two-electrode symmetric cells, such as coin-type cells (CR 2032) or Swagelok cells (MTI, Inc., USA), the slurry was applied using a spatula to a Ni foam with a 16&nbsp;mm diameter that served as a current collector (Celmet: thickness = 1.6&nbsp;mm, and mass loading range of 2–4&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), and dried at 90&nbsp;°C in a vacuum oven for 12&nbsp;h. A glass microfiber filter (Whatman GF/F) was then used as a separator. Prior to use, the Ni foam was cleaned by sonicating it for 30&nbsp;min in a 1&nbsp;M HCl solution and then washing it thoroughly with distilled de-ionized water. The procedure was repeated twice, and the Ni foam was dried overnight in a vacuum oven. Prior to testing, the cell was allowed to equilibrate for 6&nbsp;h. The electrochemical performances of cyclic voltammetry (CV), galvanostatic cycling with potential limitation (GCPL), and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were carried out at room temperature. The tests were carried out on a computer-controlled multi-channel potentiostat/galvanostat Bio-Logic VMP3 operated by EC-Lab v10.40 software. The CV tests were carried out at scan rates ranging from 5 to 100 mVs<sup>−1</sup>. The GCPL tests were performed at current densities ranging from 3 to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. EIS measurements were performed in the frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz to 10&nbsp;mHz at an open-circuit voltage with an AC voltage amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV, and data were analyzed using the Z-fit tool.</p><p>The electrochemical properties of specific capacitance, energy density, and power density were calculated from a galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curve according to the following equations. The specific capacitance (<i>C</i><sub>sp</sub>), maximum power density (<i>P</i><sub>max</sub>), and specific energy density (<i>E</i><sub>sp</sub>) for the full cells (symmetric devices) were evaluated from the slope of the charge–discharge curves using&nbsp;Eqs.&nbsp;(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Equ1">1</a>)–(<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Equ2">4</a>).</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$C\left(F\right)=\frac{i}{\Delta V/\Delta t}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${C}_{{\text{sp}}}\left(F{g}^{-1}\right)=\frac{4C}{m}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (2)
                </div></div><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${P}_{{\text{max}}}\left({10}^{3}W k{g}^{-1}\right)=\frac{{V}^{2}}{4m{R}_{s}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (3)
                </div></div><div id="Equ4" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$E\left(\frac{1}{3.6}{Wh kg}^{-1}\right)=\frac{C{V}^{2}}{2m}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (4)
                </div></div><p>where&nbsp;<i>i</i>&nbsp;(<i>A</i>) is the applied current, Δ<i>V</i>&nbsp;(V)/Δ<i>t</i>&nbsp;(s) is the slope of the discharge curve and&nbsp;<i>m</i>&nbsp;(g) is the total mass of both electrodes,&nbsp;<i>C</i>&nbsp;(<i>F</i>) is the calculated capacitance,&nbsp;<i>V</i>&nbsp;(<i>V</i>) is the maximum voltage obtained during charge, and&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>&nbsp;is the equivalent series resistance (ESR) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Raju K, Han H, Velusamy DB, Jiang Q, Yang H, Nkosi FP, Palaniyandy N, Makgopa K, Bo Z, Ozoemena KI (2020) Rational design of 2D manganese phosphate hydrate nanosheets as pseudocapacitive electrodes. ACS Energy Lett 5:23–30" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1140">64</a>].</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results and discussions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Results and discussions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig1">1</a> shows a schematic representation of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrodes. The α-Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> homogeneous spherical particle was achieved from agglomerated EMD particles. The resultant product was microwaved to obtain one-dimensional&nbsp;α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods. Finally, the 1D-α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite was developed in which OLC nanoparticles were encased α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods, which is advantageous for rapid ion transport during the electrochemistry process and has an effect on the structural stability of the composite.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure&nbsp;1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure&nbsp;1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="274"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Schematic representation of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>a depicts the wide scan XPS spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite recorded from zero to 1300&nbsp;eV. Only Mn, O, and C signals can be seen, indicating the sample purity. The calculated atomic percentage of the elements of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> are O 1&nbsp;s (54.3%), Mn 2p (27.1%), and C 1&nbsp;s (18.6%), while O 1&nbsp;s (9.7%), Mn 2p (4.8%), and C 1&nbsp;s (85.5%) for α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite. The carbon signal in the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite shows quite intense and most likely arises from the carbon present in the composite. The Mn 2p spectrum for α-MnO<sub>2</sub> is illustrated in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>b, and it consists of two major splitting peaks appearing at 642.6&nbsp;eV and 654.1&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbits, with the spin energy separation of 11.5&nbsp;eV, while Mn 2p spectrum for α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>c) involves two doublets at 642.3&nbsp;eV (Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 653.8&nbsp;eV (Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>), with the energy difference of 11.5&nbsp;eV. The energy difference values of both materials indicate the presence of a 4<sup>+</sup> oxidation state of Mn metal [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Nithyadharseni P, Reddy MV, Fanny H, Adams S, Chowdari BVR (2015) Facile one-pot synthesis and Li-cycling properties of MnO2. RSC Adv 5:60552–60561" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1231">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li W, Zeng R, Sun Z, Tian D, Dou S (2014) Uncoupled surface spin induced exchange bias in α- MnO2 nanowires. Sci Rep 4:6641" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1234">65</a>]. It is difficult to arrive at a firm conclusion of the Mn oxidation state from the Mn2p spectra because the splitting energy gap always differs for the different valence state; therefore, it is more essential to evaluate the Mn 3&nbsp;s spectra to determine the oxidation state of Mn. The Mn 3&nbsp;s spectrum of both materials is exemplified in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>d and evolves two doublet peaks located at 84.3&nbsp;eV and 88.9&nbsp;eV and 83.9&nbsp;eV and 88.7&nbsp;eV, with energy splitting gap of 4.6&nbsp;eV and 4.8&nbsp;eV for α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite, respectively. This result clearly indicates that both materials consume mainly Mn<sup>4+</sup> and with some Mn<sup>3+</sup> oxidation state of Mn [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Davoglio RA, Cabello G, Marco JF, Biaggio SR (2018) Synthesis and characterization of α- MnO2 nanoneedles for electrochemical supercapacitors. Electrochim Acta 261:428–435" href="#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1249">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Palaniyandy N, Nkosi FP, Raju K, Ozoemena KI (2019) Conversion of electrolytic MnO2 to Mn3O4 nanowires for high-performance anode materials for lithium-ion batteries. J Electroanal Chem 833:79–92" href="#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1249_1">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Cheng J, Wang D, Wang B, Ning H, Zhang Y, Li Y, An J, Gao P (2020) Plasma-catalytic degradation of ciprofloxacin in aqueous solution over different MnO2 nanocrystals in a dielectric barrier discharge system. Chemosphere 253:126595" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1253">68</a>]. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>e depicts the C1s spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite; the strongest peak at 284.8&nbsp;eV ascribed to the C–C bonds encapsulated from the OLC in the composite, while the other peaks at 285.8&nbsp;eV and 287.5&nbsp;eV are assigned to C–O and O–C=O corresponding to the presence of some oxygen-containing functional groups in the organic matrix and from a trace amount of carboxyl in the hybrid samples. O 1&nbsp;s spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>f, g) shows three different peaks at 529.8&nbsp;eV, 531.3&nbsp;eV, and 533.3&nbsp;eV and 529.6&nbsp;eV, 531.2&nbsp;eV, and 533.3&nbsp;eV. The main peak at ~ 529.8&nbsp;eV corresponds to metal oxide bonds, while the other peaks at 531.3&nbsp;eV and 533.3&nbsp;eV attributed to hydroxide and water (C–O, C=O, OH), which are good agreement with reported literature [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Hussain S, Amade R, Jover E, Bertran E (2013) Water plasma functionalized CNTs/MnO2 composites for supercapacitors. Sci World J. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1155/2013/832581
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1269">69</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Yang Z, Lv J, Pang H, Yan W, Qian K, Guo T, Guo Z (2015) Facile Synthesis of coaxial CNTs/MnO x-carbon hybrid nanofibers and their greatly enhanced lithium storage performance. Sci Rep 5:17473" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1272">70</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure&nbsp;2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure&nbsp;2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="756"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>XPS, XRD, and TG–DTA analysis of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite: (<b>a</b>) XPS broad survey spectrum of both samples, (<b>b</b>) Mn 2p spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>, (<b>c</b>) Mn 2p spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC, (<b>d</b>) Mn 3&nbsp;s spectrum of both samples, (<b>e</b>) C 1&nbsp;s spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC, (<b>f</b>) O 1&nbsp;s spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>, and (<b>g</b>) O 1&nbsp;s spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC. (<b>h</b>) XRD pattern of both samples, and (<b>i</b>) Rietveld refinement XRD pattern of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>. (<b>j</b>) TGA curves and (<b>k</b>) DTG curves of both samples.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>h depicts the X-ray diffraction pattern (XRD) of all the samples (α-MnO<sub>2</sub>, α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC, and OLC) synthesized by microwave irradiation method. XRD reveals that all the diffraction peaks of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite are well indexed to the tetragonal Hollandite-type structure of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;with the space group 14/m (87), in which the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra are linked to form double zigzag chains along the c-axis by edge-sharing. These double chains then share their corners with each other forming approximately square tunnels parallel to the c-axis, as shown in the inset of Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>i [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li W, Zeng R, Sun Z, Tian D, Dou S (2014) Uncoupled surface spin induced exchange bias in α- MnO2 nanowires. Sci Rep 4:6641" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1367">65</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Wei C, Xu C, Li B, Du H, Kang F (2012) Preparation and characterization of manganese dioxides with nano-sized tunnel structures for zinc ion storage. J Phys Chem Solids 73(12):1487–1491" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1370">71</a>]. No characteristic peaks were observed related to&nbsp;impurities&nbsp;in the XRD pattern, suggesting a pure phase of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> crystal structure. The XRD pattern of OLC is co-plotted with α-MnO<sub>2</sub> which shows two significant peaks at 25° and 43.78° associated with the (002) and (111) planes of OLC revealing the existence of graphitic carbon [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu CC, Hung CY, Chang KH, Yang YL (2011) A hierarchical nanostructure consisting of amorphous MnO2, Mn3O4 nanocrystallites, and single-crystalline MnOOH nanowires for supercapacitors. J Power Sources 196:847–850" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1378">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Makgopa K, Ejikeme PM, Jafta CJ, Raju K, Zeiger M, Presser V, Ozoemena KI (2015) A high-rate aqueous symmetric pseudocapacitor based on highly graphitized onion-like carbon/birnessite-type manganese oxide nanohybrids. J Mater Chem A 3:3480–3490" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1381">72</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Su D, Ahn HJ, Wang G (2013) Hydrothermal synthesis of α- MnO2 and β- MnO2 nanorods as high-capacity cathode materials for sodium ion batteries. J Mater Chem A 1:4845–4850" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1384">73</a>]. Including OLC in α-MnO<sub>2</sub> matrix certainly modifies the original parameters of the later due to the structural deformations of OLC. This can be confirmed by refining the XRD patterns of the pure and compared with the composite α-MnO<sub>2</sub> samples (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>i). Though lattice parameters shows similar values, crystallite size of the composite sample is smaller than that of the pristine which is completely due to the addition of OLC [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li W, Zeng R, Sun Z, Tian D, Dou S (2014) Uncoupled surface spin induced exchange bias in α- MnO2 nanowires. Sci Rep 4:6641" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1395">65</a>]. The dense yet smaller nanoparticles greatly affect the crystal density and volume of the unit cell as shown in Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Tab1">1</a>. This can be further understood by observing the peak shift and larger FWHM of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite than the pristine (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>h).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table&nbsp;1 Refinement parameters of pristine and composite α-MnO<sub>2</sub> obtained through TOPAS 3</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/tables/1" aria-label="Full size table 1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>TG–DTA study was performed to investigate the thermal stability and chemical composition of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig2">2</a>j, k)&nbsp;composite under airflow. The α-MnO<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composites have a feeble weight loss that occurred at temperatures in the range of 150 to 220&nbsp;°C, which could be attributed to the physically absorbed water molecules from the materials owing to their hygroscopicity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Petnikota S, Srikanth VVSS, Nithyadharseni P, Reddy MV, Adams S, Chowdari BVR (2015) Sustainable graphenothermal reduction chemistry to obtain MnO nanonetwork supported exfoliated graphene oxide composite and its electrochemical characteristics. ACS Sustain Chem Eng 3:3205–3213" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1564">74</a>]. All types of manganese oxide sample possess multistage decomposition with respect to temperatures, and in the present samples apart from the initial weight loss, a major weight loss event is recorded at 550–630&nbsp;°C range. Transition at this temperature range is always associated with the formation of non-stoichiometric compositions such as MnO<sub>x</sub> or phase transformations such as Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Wu W, Wang P, Lin L, Dai SF (2018) Manganese ore decomposition and carbon reduction in steelmaking. High Temp Mater Process (London) 37(8):741–747" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1573">75</a>] due to the loss of oxygen atoms. Further weight loss events lead to the other phases like Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> which are not prominently seen here. However, OLC undergo decomposition at the similar temperature range indicating the poor thermodynamic stability of carbon at higher-temperature range. Overall, the pristine sample shows lesser weight loss (~ 6%)&nbsp;compared to the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (~ 46%). As mentioned earlier, the reason for such a large amount of weight loss event must be corroborated to the existence of carbon in the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> matrix. Calculating the percentages of weight loss events, the composite sample composes 46% of carbon and another 54% as α-MnO<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;and this data goes in line with the preparatory entries. Considering the pristine sample, the smallest weight loss observed is a positive attribute of this system which is better than weight loss reports available elsewhere [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Jing FY, Pei J, Zhou Y, Shang Y, Yao S, Liu S, Chen G (2022) High-performance reversible aqueous Zinc-Ion battery based on Zn2+ pre-intercalation alpha-manganese dioxide nanowires/carbon nanotubes. J Colloid Interface Sci 609:557–565" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1587">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Zhang X, Wang T, Jiang C, Zhang F, Li W, Tang Y (2016) Manganese dioxide/carbon nanotubes composite with optimized microstructure via room temperature solution approach for high-performance lithium-ion battery anodes. Electrochim Acta 187:465–472" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1590">76</a>]. For each weight loss event, a corresponding endothermic reaction is seen in the DTA plot which infers the thermal decomposition reaction at the corresponding thermal ranges.</p><p>The morphological and EDX investigations of pristine and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>a–q. One-dimensional α-MnO<sub>2</sub> dominates the morphology of the composite matrix, whereas the pristine OLCs are amorphous and extremely small to resolve in the composite sample. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>a–d indicates the dimensions of the nanorods and the distribution of the agglomerated grains. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>a exhibits the layered/onion-like morphology of MnO<sub>2</sub> sample, while it is imperative to understand that rod-shaped particles/grains of MnO<sub>2</sub> resemble concentric structure morphology collectively. Also, EMD often exhibits rod-shaped morphology in MnO<sub>2</sub> samples which has been widely reported, while similar petal-like structures is observed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou Y, Chen T, Zhang J, Liu Y, Ren P (2017) Amorphous MnO2 as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. Chi J Chem 35:1294–1298" href="#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1617">77</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Su D, Ahn HJ, Wang G (2013) β-MnO2 nanorods with exposed tunnel structures as high-performance cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. NPG Asia Mater 5:e70" href="#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1617_1">78</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Biswal A, Chandra Tripathy BC, Sanjay K, Subbaiah T, Minakshi M (2015) Electrolytic manganese dioxide (EMD): a perspective on worldwide production, reserves and its role in electrochemistry. RSC Adv 5(72):58255–58283. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1039/C5RA05892A
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1620">79</a>]. However, dimensional analysis indicates the assorted sizes all over the matrix of pristine (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>a, b) and doped samples (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>c, d) which can be ascribed to the synthesis conditions. However, to estimate the existence of carbon samples in the samples, HRTEM analysis was performed and owing to the smaller size, and the carbon samples are not viewed legibly. Still, the settlement of amorphous carbon powders surrounding the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods can be seen through the shadow regions, in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>e, f. The SAED pattern further confirms the purity of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> sample with the characteristic d-spacing and miller indices. Finally, the elemental distribution is viewed through mapping process which reveals the existence of Mn, O, C elements alone as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig3">3</a>n–q.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure&nbsp;3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure&nbsp;3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="477"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>SEM images of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods (<b>a</b>, <b>b</b>), and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (<b>c</b>, <b>d</b>). TEM images of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> (<b>e</b>, <b>f</b>), α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (<b>i</b>, <b>j</b>, <b>k</b>) and HRTEM images of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>, and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC (<b>g</b>, <b>l</b>) and SAED pattern of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> (<b>h</b>). EDAX mapping images (<b>m</b>, <b>n</b>, <b>o</b> and <b>p</b>) and EDAX spectrum of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite&nbsp;(<b>q</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The BET findings (not shown) infer that α-MnO<sub>2</sub> exhibits a surface area of 42.2 m<sup>2</sup>/g, pore volume of 0.118 cm<sup>3</sup>/g, and a pore size of 13&nbsp;nm. Conversely, OLC-included α-MnO<sub>2</sub> displays a surface area of 45.4 m<sup>2</sup>/g, pore volume of 0.123 cm<sup>3</sup>/g, and a pore size of 11.9&nbsp;nm. Surface area of 22.5 m<sup>2</sup>/g and 12&nbsp;nm pore size has been reported for nanorod-shaped α-MnO<sub>2</sub> elsewhere which indicates the similarity of present findings in pore size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Minakshi Sundaram M, Biswal A, Mitchell DRG, Jones R, Fernandez C (2016) Correlation among physical and electrochemical behaviour of nanostructured electrolytic manganese dioxide from leach liquor and synthetic for aqueous asymmetric capacitor. Phys Chem Chem Phys 18(6):4711–4720. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1039/c5cp07141k
                  
                " href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1749">80</a>]. A similar finding has been reported elsewhere with a lower surface area [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Biswal SA, Tripathy BC, Subbaiah T, Meyrick D, Minakshi M (2015) Dual effect of anionic surfactants in the electrodeposited MnO2 trafficking redox ions for energy. J Electrochem Soc 162(1):A30–A38" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1752">81</a>]. Overall, EMD-processed α-MnO<sub>2</sub> exhibits larger surface area, while this is further large in the OLC-included sample. Both types of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> present suitable characteristics for Na- and Zn-ion batteries, providing moderate surface areas enabling ion adsorption and pore volumes facilitating ion diffusion. Larger surface area and pore volume of OLC/α-MnO<sub>2</sub> could facilitate the ion migration across the electrode and potentially boost battery performance. For supercapacitors necessitating high surface areas and pore volumes, both α-MnO<sub>2</sub> samples exhibit favorable traits and the higher surface area of OLC-included α-MnO<sub>2</sub> particularly shows the better electrochemical performance. It is important to note that mesopores of both samples facilitates the smoother charge transport kinetics across the electrode during electrochemical reactions and this is further beneficial in the SCs where surface reactions occur.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Electrochemistry analysis</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">Sodium-ion batteries</h4><p>Cyclic voltammogram of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrodes is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>a, b for the initial ten cycles. The redox peaks are found to be stable within a voltage window of 1.0–4.3&nbsp;V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. The electrodes exhibit a pair of redox peaks corresponding to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and de-insertion in the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> crystal structure: (1) between 2.5&nbsp;V and 1.5&nbsp;V; (2) between 2.6&nbsp;V and 3.5&nbsp;V. However, the peak potential values of composite electrode are found to show minute shifting compared to the pristine which is due to the addition of OLC. In addition to the minute peak shift, peak current values are also improved in the composite sample which should be marked to the OLC contribution alone [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Zhou Y, Chen T, Zhang J, Liu Y, Ren P (2017) Amorphous MnO2 as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. Chi J Chem 35:1294–1298" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1791">77</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure&nbsp;4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure&nbsp;4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs10853-024-09373-2/MediaObjects/10853_2024_9373_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="545"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Electrochemistry performance of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite in sodium-ion battery applications: CV analysis of pristine α-MnO<sub>2</sub> (<b>a</b>) and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (<b>b</b>), capacity vs. voltage profile (<b>c</b>), cyclic performance (<b>d</b>) and rate performance, (<b>e</b>) pristine α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite. Nyquist plots of (<b>f</b>) of the electrodes at open-circuit potential, after aging cycles of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (<b>g</b>, <b>h</b>) and its equivalent electronic circuit (<b>i</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The first cycle voltage profiles of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrodes, at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>c. The α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite exemplify a discharge plateau from 3.2 to 3.8&nbsp;V and 2.6 to 3.75&nbsp;V, respectively. The wide plateau area of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite than the pristine α-MnO<sub>2</sub> attributed to the increased electrochemical performance of this sample after the addition of OLC. The initial charge/discharge capacities of 192/230 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 245/266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained for α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorod and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrodes, respectively. The cycling performance of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorod and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>d) depicts a rapid capacity decay up to initial 20 cycles (shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>d inset) and attains stability after the 20 cycles and maintained up to 500 cycles. The intercalation/deintercalation reactions at the working electrode contribute to the phase/spatial changes in the electrode material which induces structural vibrations initially. After the stable interface layer formation on the electrode surface, the material exhibits smooth redox reactions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Su D, Ahn HJ, Wang G (2013) β-MnO2 nanorods with exposed tunnel structures as high-performance cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. NPG Asia Mater 5:e70" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1895">78</a>] and the same is ensured here. This interface formation enables the improved contact area and efficient percolation of the electrolyte into the electrode material. The α-MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods exhibit an initial capacity of 230 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and drop to 109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the 100th cycle and slightly reduced to 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity is maintained up to 500 cycle, whereas α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite displays an increased initial capacity of 266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and then drops to 139 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the 100th cycle and the same capacity is maintained up to the 500th cycles (139 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Compared to pristine α-MnO<sub>2</sub>, α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite exhibits a higher capacity and better cycling stability and this behavior is related to the OLC in the composite electrode. The active structural rearrangements of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> sample after OLC addition benefit more number of cation intercalation and facilitate the electrical conductivity along with rate capability. The overall capacity retention is 45.3% and 66.5% for α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrodes, respectively, after 500 cycles.</p><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>e infers the rate performances of the pristine and composite samples at different current densities (50, 100, 200, 500, and 700&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Increasing the current density induces rapid electrochemical kinetics in the system under analysis which obviously lessens the output capacity of the electrode materials. Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Tab2">2</a> indicates the current density and relative discharge capacities of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and composite samples. It is worth to emphasize that the composite sample was able to deliver a discharge capacity 137 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density 700&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after undergoing the deep cycles of 500 numbers at a slow scan rate 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This behavior is commendable than the rate performance of the pristine material and other reported electrodes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Zhou Y, Chen T, Zhang J, Liu Y, Ren P (2017) Amorphous MnO2 as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. Chi J Chem 35:1294–1298" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1947">77</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang Y, Yang Y, Zhang Y, Zhang T, Ye M (2012) Heterogeneous oxidation of naproxen in the presence of α-MnO2&nbsp;nanostructures with different morphologies. Appl Cat B 127:182–189. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1016/j.apcatb.2012.08.014
                  
                " href="#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1950">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yusof SN, Zain NK, Misnon II, Jose R (2018) Electrochemical evaluation of fluorinated MnO2 for supercapacitor application. MATEC Web Conf 150:02006. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1051/matecconf/201815002006
                  
                " href="#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1950_1">83</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pandit B, Rondiya SR, Dzade NY, Shaikh SF, Kumar N, Goda ES, Al-Kahtani AA, Mane RS, Mathur S, Salunkhe RR (2021) High stability and long cycle life of rechargeable sodium-ion battery using manganese oxide cathode: a combined density functional theory (DFT) and experimental study. ACS Appl Mater Interfaces 13:11433–11441" href="#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1950_2">84</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang Z, Yan XD, Wang F, Xiong T, Balogun MS, Zhou H, Deng J (2021) Reduced graphene oxide thin layer induced lattice distortion in high crystalline MnO2 nanowires for high-performance sodium- and potassium-ion batteries and capacitors. Carbon 174:556–566" href="#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1950_3">85</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang R, Liu Z, Zhao D, Xu J, Cao Y, Huang JH, Zhang J (2022) In situ micro-current collector of amorphous manganese dioxide as cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. Ionics 28:1211–1217" href="#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1950_4">86</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Wu L, Guo S, Pu X, Yue H, Li H, Li P, Li W, Cai K, Ding W, Li L, Zhang Y, Fa W, Yang C, Zheng Z, He WW, Cao Y (2022) Na0.91 MnO2 with an extended layer structure and excellent pseudocapacitive behavior as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. ACS Appl Energy Mater 5:4505–4512" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e1953">87</a>].</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table&nbsp;2 Rate performances of the pristine and composite electrodes for SIBs</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2/tables/2" aria-label="Full size table 2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In order to investigate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the electrode, EIS tests were conducted at open-circuit potential, after 2 cycles and after aging at a charge (4.3&nbsp;V) and discharge (1.0 V) and the Nyquist plots of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite are given in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>f, g, and h. The Nyquist plots (Z’ vs. Z’’) of all the composites were fitted using an electrical equivalent circuit (EEC) given in the inset of Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>i, in which the dots represent the experimental data and the lines denote the calculated data. The Nyquist plots of all compositions consist of one or two semicircles in the high-mid frequency region, represented by surface resistance due to the SEI contribution (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) followed by an inclined line in the low-frequency region, represented by Warburg impedance (<i>W</i>s). The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite electrode is (849.4), which is significantly lower than the value of α-MnO<sub>2</sub> (1043.2), indicating that the α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite has a faster charge transfer process. The morphology of the OLC can contribute to the better electron conduction during charge and discharge, and it is true that the charge transfer process of α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC has improved in comparison with α-MnO<sub>2</sub>. However, prolonged cycles throw different behaviors in impedance patterns of both the samples. It is obvious to decrease in the SEI-related impedances after a few numbers of cycling due to the stable interlayer formation. When the number of cycles is increased to larger units, due to the structural transformations, overall cell resistance increases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Li H, Liu A, Zhao S, Guo Z, Wang N, Ma T (2018) In Situ growth of a feather-like MnO2 nanostructure on carbon paper for high-performance rechargeable sodium-ion batteries. ChemElectroChem 5:3266–3272" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d64489916e2150">88</a>] and the same holds true in the present electrodes, in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig4">4</a>g, h.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Zinc-ion batteries</h4><p>The CV pattern of the α-MnO<sub>2</sub> and α-MnO<sub>2</sub>/OLC composite&nbsp;(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s10853-024-09373-2#Fig5">5</a>a, b) electrodes shows the two pair of redox peaks, while the pristine sample’s oxidation peak is broadened by accommodating two peaks.
